Obligations of Jahr and Isrār for the Imam in Various Prayers
Lists the prayers where jahr and isrār are wājib for the Imam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the prayers in which jahr and isrār are wājib for the Imam, and explain the reasoning based on the Prophet's constant practice and the Qur'anic verse.

Core Idea
Jahr and isrār are wājib for the Imam based on the Prophet's constant practice and consensus; the munfarid has choice.

This passage details the Hanafi ruling on when an Imam must recite aloud (jahr) and silently (isrār) in prayer. It specifies obligatory loud recitation in Fajr, the first two rak'ahs of Maghrib and 'Ishā', Jumu'ah, Eid, Tarāwīh, and Witr in Ramadān, and silent recitation in Zuhr, 'Asr, and the remaining rak'ahs. The reasoning is rooted in the Prophet's practice after the revelation of Qur'an 17:110, and the ruling persists despite the original cause ceasing. Understanding these obligations is crucial for an Imam leading congregational prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what is being ruled upon: the mode of recitation (jahr or isrār) for the Imam in specific prayers. The ruling level is wājib.
Wājib means obligatory; omission requires compensation (sajdah al-sahw) but prayer is still valid.
2
Locate the dalil
The text cites Qur'an 17:110 and the Prophet's constant practice (muwāẓabah) as evidence. Identify the verse and the prophetic actions as sources.
The Qur'anic verse was revealed to moderate recitation; the Prophet then consistently recited aloud in certain prayers and silently in others.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the dalil proves wujūb. The Hanafi school holds that the Prophet's continuous practice on a specific manner indicates obligation, especially when combined with consensus.
The verse itself does not command wujūb explicitly, but the Prophet's implementation and subsequent consensus establish it as wājib.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Other schools (e.g., Shāfi‘īs) consider jahr and isrār as sunnah, not wājib. Understand that this is a legitimate difference.
The Hanafi position is based on the principle that constant practice (muwāẓabah) indicates wujūb, while others see it as sunnah mu'akkadah.
5
Note the exception for munfarid
A person praying alone (munfarid) has the choice to recite aloud or silently in prayers where the Imam recites aloud. This is because the ruling of jahr/isrār is tied to being Imam.
The munfarid is not bound by the same obligation; he may choose based on his own preference or to aid concentration.

And loud recitation of the Imam in the recitation of Fajr, and the first two of the two evening prayers (Maghrib and 'Ishā'), even if make-up, and Jumu'ah, the two Eids, Tarāwīh, and Witr in Ramadan; and silent recitation in Zuhr, 'Asr, and in what is after the first two of the two evening prayers, and supererogatory prayers of the day; and the individual prays is given choice in what is recited aloud. (And) loud recitation of the Imam in the two rak'ahs of Fajr and the recitation of the first two of the two evening prayers is obligatory, due to constant practice upon it, even in make-up prayer, because the Prophet (peace be upon him) did that in the make-up. (And) loud recitation is obligatory in Jumu'ah, the two Eids, Tarāwīh, and Witr in Ramadan upon the Imam, due to constant practice. And the reality of loud recitation (jahr) is making others hear. (And) silent recitation (isrār) is obligatory, which is causing oneself to hear according to the correct view, as has preceded, in all rak'ahs of Zuhr and 'Asr, even if combined at 'Arafah, and silent recitation in what is after the first two of the two evening prayers, i.e., the third rak'ah of Maghrib and the third and fourth of 'Ishā', and silent recitation in the supererogatory prayers of the day, due to constant practice upon that. And the individual prays is given choice in what the Imam recites aloud, i.e., Maghrib, 'Ishā', Fajr, and what was mentioned before of Jumu'ah, the two Eids, and others. The basis for that is that the Prophet (peace be upon him) used to recite aloud in all prayers initially, and the polytheists would harm him and revile the One who sent it and the one upon whom it was sent. So Allah revealed: 'And do not recite aloud in your prayer nor in silence, but seek between that a way' (al-Isrā' 17:110), meaning: do not recite aloud in all of your prayer nor in silence in all of it, but seek a middle way: recite aloud in the night prayer and silently in the day prayer. Thereafter he would recite silently in Zuhr and 'Asr because they were ready to harm during these times, and he would recite aloud in Maghrib due to their busyness with eating, in 'Ishā' and Fajr due to their sleeping, and in Jumu'ah and the two Eids because he established them in Medina where the disbelievers had no power. This excuse, although it has ceased with the dominance of Muslims, the ruling remains, because its continuance does not require the continuance of the cause, and because it has substituted another excuse: the great preoccupation of people in these two prayers (Zuhr and 'Asr) unlike others. And consensus has been established on loud recitation in what we have mentioned. And the Imam should not exaggerate in loud recitation because it is disliked to exceed.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This extensive passage details the obligations of the Imam regarding the mode of recitation: jahr (loud) and isrār (silent). It is wājib for the Imam to recite aloud in Fajr, the first two rak'ahs of Maghrib and 'Ishā', Jumu'ah, Eid, Tarāwīh, and Witr during Ramaḍān. Even if these prayers are made up (qaḍā'), the recitation remains loud. Conversely, silent recitation is wājib in all rak'ahs of Zuhr and 'Asr (even when combined at 'Arafa), in the third rak'ah of Maghrib, the third and fourth of 'Ishā', and all supererogatory prayers performed during the day. A person praying alone (munfarid) has the choice to recite aloud or silently in the prayers where the Imam recites aloud. The historical reason is given: the Prophet initially recited aloud in all prayers, but when the polytheists harassed him, the verse from Sūrat al-Isrā' allowed differentiation. The ruling remains even though the original cause is gone, because it is supported by consensus and another reason (people's preoccupation during the day). The author also notes that the Imam should not shout excessively.

Key Terms
الجهر (al-jahr): Loud recitation that makes others hear.
الإسرار (al-isrār): Silent recitation that is audible to oneself.
المنفرد (al-munfarid): One who prays alone, not in congregation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) The third rak'ah of 'Ishā'
D) All rak'ahs of 'Asr

Key Takeaway
The Imam must recite aloud in Fajr, first two of Maghrib and 'Ishā', Jumu'ah, Eid, Tarāwīh, and Witr in Ramadan; silently elsewhere; the munfarid has choice.

The Wujub of the Opening Takbir and the Eid Ruku Takbir
Ruling with proof on opening takbir and Eid ruku takbir

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the opening takbir and the second rak'ah's ruku takbir in Eid prayer, and identify the evidence from the Prophet's constant practice.

Core Idea
The opening takbir is wajib for every prayer; the ruku takbir in the second rak'ah of Eid is wajib.

In this passage, the author clarifies two rulings on takbir in prayer. First, the opening takbir (takbirat al-iftitah) is wajib for every obligatory and nafl prayer, not just the Eid prayer. Second, the takbir for ruku in the second rak'ah of Eid prayer is also wajib. The proof is the Prophet's constant practice (muwazabah), which in Hanafi usul indicates wujub.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
Locate in the text the two acts that are declared wajib: the opening takbir for every prayer, and the takbir for ruku in the second rak'ah of the two Eid prayers.
The passage first discusses the opening takbir generally, then specifically for the second rak'ah of Eid.
2
Find the dalil (proof)
The text cites 'muwazabah' (constant practice) of the Prophet ﷺ as the evidence for both rulings. Note that this is a zanni dalil (probative but not certain).
The word 'مواظبة' appears in the Arabic.
3
Note the ikhtilaf (disagreement)
The passage mentions that some scholars, including al-Sarakhsi, held that it is not disliked to begin with other than takbir, but the preferred view is that it is makruh. Also, the opening takbir is not specific to Eid prayers, contrary to a minority view.
This intra-Hanafi and cross-madhhab disagreement shows the importance of the mufta bihi position.
4
Understand the rationale for the second takbir
The ruku takbir in the second rak'ah is wajib because it follows the additional takbirs of Eid prayer. The first rak'ah's ruku takbir is not wajib in this special sense.
The text says 'تبعة لتكبيرات العيدين' (following the takbirs of Eid).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتعيين التكبير لافتتاح كلِّ صلاة، لا العيدين خاصة. وتكبيرةُ الرّكوعِ في ثانية العيدين. يجب (تعيينُ) لفظ (التكبير لافتتاح كل صلاة للمواظبة عليه، ولذا قال في « الذخيرة»: إنـه يكره الشروع بغير التكبير في الأصح. ولذا قلنا: ( لا ) يتعين التكبير لافتتـاح صـلاة (العيدين خاصة)، احترازاً عما قال بعضهم، وإليه يشير قول السرخسي: الأصح أنه لا يـكـره الـشـروع بـغـيـر التكبير، كما في « التبيين ووجه ما ذكرناه مواظبة النبي الله على التكبير عند افتتاح كل صلاة. And the specific determination of the takbir for the opening of every prayer, not specifically for the two Eids. And the takbir of ruku in the second of the two Eids. Thus we said: (Not) is the takbir specifically required for opening the prayer (of the two Eids specifically), as a precaution against what some said. And to it alludes the saying of al-Sarakhsi: The most correct is that it is not disliked to begin with other than the takbir, as in al-Tabyin. And the justification for what we mentioned is the constancy of the Prophet ﷺ upon the takbir at the opening of every prayer. (And) obligatory (is the takbir of ruku in the second: i.e., the second rak'ah of (the two Eids) following the additional takbirs of the two Eids because it comes after them, contrary to the takbir of ruku in the first rak'ah of the two Eids.)
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage states the ruling on takbir for opening prayer and for ruku in Eid prayer. The author begins by confirming that the takbir (Allahu Akbar) for opening the prayer is wajib for every prayer, not just for the two Eids, contrary to a minority view that it is only for Eids. The constant practice of the Prophet ﷺ proves its obligatoriness. However, it is not wajib to pronounce 'Allahu Akbar' specifically; anyone who begins with another dhikr is not sinful in the Hanafi school. The correct view is that it is makruh to start with other than takbir. As for the takbir for ruku in the second rak'ah of Eid, it is also wajib because it follows the additional takbirs of Eid prayer. This applies to the second rak'ah only; the first rak'ah's ruku takbir is not wajib in this sense.

Key Terms
تعيين التكبير (ta'yin al-takbir): The specific utterance of 'Allahu Akbar' at the beginning of prayer
تكبيرة الركوع (takbirat al-rukū'): The takbir said while moving into ruku
مواظبة (muwāzabah): Constant practice of the Prophet ﷺ, used as evidence for wujub

The opening takbir is wajib for every prayer, and the ruku takbir in the second rak'ah of Eid is wajib, both established by the Prophet's constant practice.

Jahr and Israr: When the Imam Recites Loudly or Quietly
Ruling on when the Imam must recite loudly and quietly

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the specific prayers where the Imam must recite loudly (jahr) and quietly (israr), and explain the Hanafi proof from the Quran and Sunnah.

Core Idea
The Imam's jahr and israr are wajib, not optional, based on the Prophet's constant practice.

In this lesson, we examine the rulings on the Imam's recitation volume during prayer. The Hanafi school holds that jahr (loud recitation) and israr (quiet recitation) are obligatory (wajib) on the Imam in specific prayers, not merely recommended. The basis is the Prophet's constant practice (muwazabah) and the Qur'anic verse 17:110, as interpreted through his actions. Understanding these rules ensures the Imam fulfills a key wajib of prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the act being ruled upon is the Imam's recitation volume during specific prayers. The ruling is that jahr and israr are wajib (obligatory) on the Imam in the listed prayers.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary proof is the Prophet's constant practice (muwazabah) and the consensus (ijma) on these rulings. The verse 17:110 is also cited as the origin of the differentiation.
The verse itself commands moderation, but the Prophet's application specifies the prayers.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Hanafi school uses the Prophet's interpretation and practice as the authoritative explanation of the verse. The constant practice (muwazabah) indicates wujub (obligation).
The verse is general; the Sunnah particularizes it.
4
Note the historical context
Initially the Prophet recited loudly in all prayers, but after the verse, he differentiated based on the situation of the polytheists. The ruling remained even after the cause ceased, and another wisdom (people's occupation) sustains it.
The excuse changed but the ruling remains.
5
Summarize the list of prayers
Jahr is wajib in Fajr, first two of Maghrib and Isha (including qada), Jumu'ah, Eid, Tarawih, and Witr in Ramadan. Israr is wajib in Zuhr, Asr, remaining rak'ahs of Maghrib/Isha, and daytime nafl.
Memorize the specific prayers.

And the loud recitation of the Imam for the recitation of Fajr, and the first two of the two evenings (Maghrib and Isha), even if make-up (qada), and Jumu'ah, and the two Eids, and Tarawih, and Witr in Ramadan, and the quiet recitation. (And) obligatory (is the loud recitation of the Imam: for recitation) of the two rak'ahs (of Fajr and) recitation (of the first two of the two evenings) due to constancy upon it, and even if make-up because the Prophet ﷺ did that in make-up. (And) obligatory is loud recitation in the prayers of Jumu'ah, the two Eids, Tarawih, and Witr in Ramadan) upon the Imam due to constancy. And the reality of loud recitation: making others hear. (And) obligatory (is quiet recitation) which is: making oneself hear according to the correct view, as preceded (in) all rak'ahs (of Zuhr and Asr) even if combining them at Arafat, (and) quiet recitation (in what comes after the first two of the two evenings) which are: the third rak'ah of Maghrib and the third and fourth of Isha, (and) quiet recitation in (optional daytime prayers) due to constancy upon that. And the origin of that is that the Prophet ﷺ used to recite loudly in all prayers initially, and the polytheists used to harm him and revile the One who sent it down and the one upon whom it was sent down. So Allah revealed: 'And do not be loud in your prayer nor quiet therein' [al-Isra 17:110], i.e., do not be loud in all your prayers nor be quiet in all of them, but seek between that a way—that you be loud in the night prayer and quiet in the day prayer. So after that he would be quiet in Zuhr and Asr, because they were ready to harm at those two times, and he would be loud in Maghrib because they were occupied with eating, and in Isha and Fajr because of their sleeping, and in Jumu'ah and the two Eids because he established them in Madinah and the disbelievers had no power there. And this excuse, even if it has ceased with the dominance of the Muslims, the ruling remains, because its continuation does not depend on the continuation of the cause, and because another excuse has taken its place, namely the great occupation of people in these two prayers (Zuhr and Asr) unlike others. And consensus has been established on loud recitation in what we mentioned. And the Imam should not exaggerate in loudness because it is disliked to increase it beyond the need of the people.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage details the rulings on when the Imam must recite loudly (jahr) and quietly (israr) in prayer. The author lists the prayers for which jahr is wajib: Fajr, the first two rak'ahs of Maghrib and Isha (including make-up prayers), Jumu'ah, Eid, Tarawih, and Witr in Ramadan. The proof is the Prophet's constant practice (muwazabah) and also the consensus (ijma) on these prayers. The definition of jahr is making others hear. Israr (quiet recitation) is wajib in Zuhr, Asr (even when combined at Arafat), the remaining rak'ahs of Maghrib and Isha (third and fourth), and all daytime nafl prayers. The historical background explains the wisdom behind the differentiation: initially the Prophet recited loudly in all prayers, but after the revelation of verse 17:110, he distinguished between loud and quiet prayers based on the situation of the polytheists. Even after Muslims gained dominance, the ruling remained, and another wisdom (people's busyness during Zuhr and Asr) supports it. The Imam should not be excessively loud, as it may disturb others.

Key Terms
الجهر (al-jahr): Loud recitation that makes others hear
الإسرار (al-isrār): Quiet recitation that only makes oneself hear
المواظبة (al-muwāẓabah): The Prophet's constant practice, indicating wujub

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) All three
D) First and third

Key Takeaway
The Imam must recite loudly in Fajr, first two of Maghrib/Isha, Jumu'ah, Eid, Tarawih, and Witr in Ramadan; quietly in Zuhr, Asr, remaining rak'ahs, and daytime nafl—all wajib due to the Prophet's constant practice.

The Mufarrid's Freedom in Recitation Volume
Ruling with a comparative example and ethical condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for a person praying alone regarding loud and quiet recitation, and identify the condition of not disturbing others.

Core Idea
A person praying alone may choose recitation volume freely, like night voluntary prayer, but must not harm sleepers.

This passage clarifies the freedom of the solitary worshipper in choosing recitation volume. Unlike the Imam who must recite loudly in certain prayers, the mufarrid has full discretion. The ruling is analogous to the optional night prayer, where one may alternate between loud and quiet. The text also includes an important ethical limit: the recitation should not be so loud as to disturb sleeping people, based on the Prophet's practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and its ruling
The passage states that 'al-munfarid' (the one praying alone) is given a choice (mukhayyar) in prayers normally recited aloud by the Imam. Note the list: Maghrib, Isha, Fajr, and others like Jumu'ah and Eid.
2
Compare to the night voluntary prayer
The mufarrid's freedom is likened to that of a person performing voluntary night prayer (mutanaffil bi-al-layl), who may choose to recite loudly or quietly.
This analogy indicates that the rule is general: any solitary prayer gives choice, regardless of the time.
3
Apply the condition of moderation
The passage adds that the mufarrid should suffice with the minimum of loudness (adna al-jahr) to achieve the purpose, and must not recite so loudly as to harm a sleeper. This is derived from the hadith of Aisha describing the Prophet's practice.
The hadith indicates a balance: loud enough for those awake but not waking sleepers.
4
Confirm the absence of obligation
The key point is that the mufarrid is not obliged to recite loudly or quietly; he has full choice. This distinguishes him from the Imam, who must recite loudly in specific prayers.

Source Text

Thus in M'araj al-Dirayah.

He may choose to recite loudly or quietly as he wishes, similar to the optional night prayer. The text also advises moderation: he should not be excessively loud to disturb sleepers. The hadith from Aisha (reported in fiqh works) indicates that the Prophet's practice in night prayer was to be audible enough for those awake but not to wake the sleeping.

Making Up the Missed Surah in the Last Rak'ahs of Isha
Ruling on reciting a missed sura in later rak'ahs with Fatiha loudly

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when the Imam omits the surah in the first two rak'ahs of Isha and explain the reasoning behind making it up in the last two rak'ahs with Fatiha and jahr.

Core Idea
A missed wajib (surah) in prayer must be made up; the remedy for the Imam is to recite both Fatiha and surah loudly in the last rak'ahs.

This lesson addresses a specific scenario in congregational prayer: the Imam forgets to recite a surah after Fatiha in the first two rak'ahs of Isha. The Hanafi school provides a detailed remedy, balancing the wajib nature of the surah recitation with the principles of loud and quiet recitation. We will explore the relied-upon position and the internal Hanafi differences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act in question is an Imam who omits the surah in the first two rak'ahs of Isha (or Maghrib). The ruling is that he must make it up in the last rak'ahs by reciting both Fatiha and the surah loudly, according to the most correct view.
The same applies if he omits it in all of the first two rak'ahs, not just one.
2
Recognize the wajib nature of the original surah
In the Hanafi school, reciting a surah after Fatiha in the first two rak'ahs is wajib (obligatory). Since it was missed, it must be compensated (qada') in the subsequent rak'ahs.
The remedy is also wajib; it is not merely recommended.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
There are three positions: (a) the most correct (al-asahh): recite both Fatiha and the surah loudly; (b) Ibn Sam'ah from Abu Hanifa: recite only the surah loudly, not Fatiha; (c) Hisham from Muhammad: do not recite the surah at all in the last rak'ahs. The correct view is justified by the need to avoid combining jahr and israr in one rak'ah.
The tarjih is based on the principle of prioritizing the wajib over the nafl.
4
Understand the justification for reciting both loudly
Since the first two rak'ahs are recited loudly (jahr) and the last two quietly (israr), combining both in one rak'ah is not legislated. The lighter change is to alter the quality of the nafl act (Fatiha) from quiet to loud rather than omitting the wajib (surah). Thus, both are recited loudly.
Fakhr al-Islam preferred Ibn Sam'ah's view (only surah loudly) but the asahh holds that Fatiha becomes the 'nafl act' whose quality is changed.
5
Apply to the order of recitation and similar cases
The correct order in the last rak'ahs is Fatiha first, then the surah. Some scholars say surah first. If the Imam remembers the missing Fatiha after reciting the surah but before ruku, he recites Fatiha and then repeats the surah. This is analogous to remembering a surah during ruku.
For Maghrib, if surah is missed in the first two, it is made up in the third rak'ah with jahr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولـو تـرك السُّورةَ في أُولَيَي العِشَاءِ، قرأها في الأخريين، مع الفاتِحَةِ، جهراً. (ولـو تــرك السورة في ) ركعة من أولى المغرب أو في جميع (أُولَي العِشَاءِ، قَرَأَهَا) أي: السورة الإمام وجوباً على الأصح، واستحباباً على ما قال في الأصل ( في الأَخْرَيَيْنِ ) من العشاء، وفي الثالثـة مـن المـغــرب (مَـعَ الفَاتِحَةِ، جَهْرَاً) بهما على الأصح. وروى ابن سماعة عن أبي حنيفة: أنه يجهر بالسورة لا الفاتحة ولا يلزم الجمع بين المخافتة والجهر في ركعة، لأن القراءة تلتحق بمحل الأداء واختاره فخـر الإسلام، وروى هشام عن محمد أنه لا . أصلاً، أما الفاتحة فلوجوب إخفائها، وأما السورة فلئلا يجمع بين الجهر يجهر والمخافتة في ركعة واحدة وهو غير مشروع، ووجه الصحيح أن الجمع بين الجهر والمخافتة لمـا كـان غير مشروع في ركعة واحدة صرنا إلى أخف الأمرين، وهو تغيير صفة النفل، وهو الفاتحة دون تغيير صفة الواجب وهو السورة، ويقدم الفاتحة ثم يقرأ السورة، وهو الأشبه وعند بعضهم يقدم السورة، لأنها ملحقة بالأوليين فكان تقديمها أولى، وعند بعضهم يترك الفاتحة؛ لأنها غير واجبة، ولو تذكر الفاتحة بعد قراءة السورة قبل الركوع يأتي بها ويعيد السورة في ظاهر المذهب؛ لأنه إذا أتى بها تكون فرضاً كالسورة، فصار كما لو تذكر السورة في الركوع فإنه يأتي بها ويعيد الركوع
And if he left out the sura in the first two of the Isha, he recites it in the last two, with the Fatiha, loudly. (And if he left out the sura in) a rak'ah of the first of Maghrib or in all (of the first of Isha, he recites it) i.e., the sura, the Imam, obligatorily according to the most correct, and recommended according to what is said in al-Usul (in the last two) of Isha, and in the third of Maghrib (with the Fatiha, loudly) for both according to the most correct. And Ibn Sam'ah narrated from Abu Hanifa: that he recites loudly the sura but not the Fatiha, and it is not necessary to combine quietness and loudness in one rak'ah, because the recitation is attached to the place of performance, and Fakhr al-Islam chose this. And Hisham narrated from Muhammad that he does not... at all. As for the Fatiha, because it is obligatory to hide it, and as for the sura, so as not to combine loudness and quietness in one rak'ah, which is not legislated. And the justification for the correct view is that since combining loudness and quietness in one rak'ah is not legislated, we resort to the lighter of the two matters, which is changing the quality of the voluntary (nafl), which is the Fatiha, without changing the quality of the obligatory, which is the sura. And he gives precedence to the Fatiha then recites the sura, and this is more fitting. And according to some, he gives precedence to the sura because it is attached to the first two, so its precedence is better. And according to some, he leaves out the Fatiha because it is not obligatory. And if he remembers the Fatiha after reciting the sura before ruku, he recites it and repeats the sura in the apparent madhhab; because when he recites it, it becomes obligatory like the sura, so it is as if he remembered the sura in ruku—he recites it and repeats the ruku.

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling when the Imam forgets to recite a sura (additional chapter) after Fatiha in the first two rak'ahs of Isha (first two of Maghrib analogous). The relied-upon position (al-asahh) is that he must recite the missed sura in the last two rak'ahs, along with Fatiha, and do so loudly. There is a difference of opinion: Ibn Sam'ah's narration from Abu Hanifa says he should only recite the sura loudly, not the Fatiha, to avoid combining loud and quiet in one rak'ah. Fakhr al-Islam chose this. Another narration from Hisham from Muhammad says he does not recite the sura at all. The correct view (asahh) is that since combining jahr and israr in one rak'ah is not permitted, the lighter change is to alter the quality of the nafl (Fatiha is not wajib in last rak'ahs) rather than the wajib (sura recitation is wajib in first two, so it is made up). Thus, he recites Fatiha and sura loudly. There is also discussion about the order: most say Fatiha first, then sura; some prefer sura first. If he remembers the Fatiha after the sura but before ruku, he recites it and repeats the sura, similar to remembering a sura during ruku.

Key Terms
أولى العشاءين (ūlayay al-‘ishā’ayn): The first two rak'ahs of Maghrib and Isha
الأخريين (al-ukhayrayn): The last two rak'ahs of Isha or the third of Maghrib

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When the Imam misses the surah in the first rak'ahs of Isha, what is the relied-upon Hanafi ruling?
A) He recites only Fatiha loudly in the last two
B) He recites the surah only loudly in the last two
C) He recites both Fatiha and surah loudly in the last two
D) He does not recite the surah at all

Key Takeaway
An omitted wajib surah in the first two rak'ahs must be made up in the last rak'ahs by reciting both Fatiha and surah loudly, according to the preferred Hanafi position.

Missing Fatiha in First Two Rak'ahs: No Repetition, Only Sajdah Sahw
Ruling on not repeating Fatiha in last rak'ahs if missed in first two

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when Fatiha is omitted in the first two rak'ahs and explain the reasoning that prevents its repetition in the last two.

Core Idea
If Fatiha is missed in the first two, do not repeat it in the last two; compensate with sajdah sahw.

In the Hanafi school, reciting Surah al-Fatiha is a wajib in the first two rak'ahs of every prayer. What happens if the imam forgets it? A common misconception is that he should make it up in the last two. The text clarifies that this is not the case: he leaves it and performs prostration of forgetfulness. The reasoning involves the nature of Fatiha's obligation and the status of the last two rak'ahs.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling
When Fatiha is omitted in the first two rak'ahs of a four-rak'ah prayer, what does the relied-upon Hanafi position require?
The answer: do not repeat Fatiha in the last two; perform sajdah sahw.
2
Identify the basis
Why is Fatiha not repeated? The text says because recitation of Fatiha in the second pair (last two) is prescribed as voluntary (nafl), not as an obligation. Repeating it would violate that prescription.
Key principle: the last two are not the proper place for the obligatory Fatiha.
3
Distinguish from sura
Why can a missed sura (after Fatiha) be made up in the last two? Because the sura is not wajib in the last two; it is voluntary there, so making it up as qada is permissible.
The difference lies in whether the recitation is wajib or nafl in the last two.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
What does al-Atabi's narration say? He holds that repeating Fatiha in nafl is not disliked due to a report (khabar). The text presents this as a minority view.
The relied-upon view is the opposite: repetition is disliked.
5
Evaluate the relied-upon view
Which view is mufta bihi? The view of no repetition, as it is the zahir riwayah and the dominant position in the school.
Al-Atabi's view is not adopted for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولـو تـرك الفاتحة لا يُكرَّرُهَا فِي الْأُخْرَيَيْنِ. (ولـو تـرك الفاتحة) في الأوليين ( لا يُكررها في الأُخْرَيَيْن) عندهم، ويسجد للسهو؛ لأن قراءة الفاتحة في الشـفـع الـثـــاني مشروعة كقراءة السورة فيه مشروعة نفلاً أيضاً، على ما في شرح الجامع الصغير» لفخر الإسلام، فإذا قرأ الفاتحة مرة وقع عن الأداء لأنها أقوى لكونها في محلها، ولو كررهـا خــالـف المشروع، بخلاف السورة فإن الشفع الثاني ليس محلاً لها أداءً، فجاز أن تقع قضاء لكن ذكر العتابي في « فتاواه » أن تكرار الفاتحة في التطوع لا يكره لورود الخبر في مثله (۳) فإن قيل: القضاء صرف مـا لـه لمـا عـلـيـه وقـد شرعت الفاتحة في الأخريين حقاً له فله صرفه إلى ما عليه فيقضيها والسورة لم تشرع في الأخرييـن حقـاً له فلا يقضيها، قلنا: على رواية الحسن عن أبي حنيفة رحمه الله : قراءة الفاتحة واجبة في الأخريين، فلم يملك صرفها إلى ما عليه؛ لأنها لم تشرع حقاً له، وأما السورة فشرعت نفلاً في الأخريين حتى لو قرأها فيهما بعد الفاتحة لم يجب عليه سجود السهو فملك صرفها إلى ما عليه.
And if he left out the Fatiha, he does not repeat it in the last two. (And if he left out the Fatiha) in the first two (he does not repeat it in the last two) according to them, and he performs sajdah sahw. Because recitation of Fatiha in the second pair (last two) is prescribed as recitation of the sura therein is also prescribed as voluntary (nafl), according to what is in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir of Fakhr al-Islam. So if he recites the Fatiha once, it counts as performance because it is stronger due to being in its place, and if he repeats it, he violates what is prescribed. Contrary to the sura, because the second pair is not a place for it in terms of performance (ada'), so it may occur as make-up (qada'). But al-Atabi mentioned in his Fatawa that repeating the Fatiha in voluntary prayer is not disliked due to the report in its like. So if it is said: 'Make-up is the diversion of something from its proper time to its due time. The Fatiha has been prescribed in the last two as a right for him, so he can divert it to its due time and make it up, while the sura has not been prescribed in the last two as a right for him, so he cannot make it up.' We say: According to the riwaya of Hasan from Abu Hanifa: Recitation of Fatiha is obligatory in the last two, so he does not have the right to divert it to its due time, because it was not prescribed as a right for him. As for the sura, it is prescribed as voluntary in the last two, such that if he recites it there after the Fatiha, sajdah sahw is not required, so he has the right to divert it to its due time.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage deals with the case where the Imam forgets to recite Fatiha in the first two rak'ahs of a prayer (like Isha). The ruling is that he does not repeat it in the last two rak'ahs; instead, he performs sajdah sahw (prostration of forgetfulness). The reason is that reciting Fatiha in the last two is a voluntary (nafl) act, not obligatory. If he recites it there once, it counts as the required Fatiha for the first two, but the correct view is that it is not a substitute; rather, he leaves it and compensates with sajdah sahw. A contrary narration from al-Atabi says repetition in nafl is not disliked. The passage also clarifies a subtle point: in the last two, Fatiha is prescribed as an obligation according to one riwaya from Abu Hanifa (via Hasan), but the dominant view is that it is not wajib. In that riwaya, he cannot make up the missed Fatiha in the last two because it is already prescribed there as an obligation; making it up would interfere with its own obligation. The sura, however, is not prescribed as a wajib in the last two, so it can be made up as qada. The discussion shows the depth of Hanafi reasoning regarding the nature of qada.

Key Terms
سجود السهو (sujūd al-sahw): Prostration of forgetfulness performed to compensate for an unintentional omission of a wajib element
الشفع الثاني (al-shaf‘ al-thānī): The second pair of rak'ahs in a four-rak'ah prayer (i.e., the last two)
أداء (adā'): Performance of an act in its prescribed time and manner (original place)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi view, if the imam forgets Fatiha in the first two rak'ahs of Isha, what is the required action?
A) Repeat Fatiha in the last two
B) Perform sajdah sahw only
C) Repeat the entire prayer
D) Recite a sura instead

Key Takeaway
When a wajib element (Fatiha) is omitted in its prescribed place (first two rak'ahs), it is not made up in a different place; instead, sajdah sahw compensates.

Recitation of Omitted Surah in Later Rak'ahs: Dispute on Loud vs. Soft
Intra-Hanafi dispute on jahr/ikhfa for reciting omitted surah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on reciting the omitted surah in the last two rak'ahs, explain the intra-madhhab dispute regarding jahr/ikhfa, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
When a surah is omitted, the correct view is to recite both Fatiha and the surah aloud.

This passage from Imdad al-Fattah tackles a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute: when an imam omits the surah in the first two rak'ahs of Maghrib or Isha, how should he recite in the later rak'ahs? The base text states that he recites the omitted surah along with Fatiha, and does so aloud. However, within the Hanafi school, there are divergent narrations from Abu Hanifa and Muhammad regarding whether to recite aloud and which part to emphasize. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to navigate the Hanafi tradition's internal methodology and identify the relied-upon (mufta bihi) view.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the core ruling from the base text: if the surah is omitted in the first two rak'ahs, it must be recited in the last two along with Fatiha, and both are recited aloud.
This is the default ruling of the madhhab.
2
Identify the dispute on jahr/ikhfa
Recognize the three views within the Hanafi school: (1) Abu Hanifa via Ibn Sama'a: recite only the surah aloud, not Fatiha; (2) Muhammad via Hisham: do not recite aloud at all; (3) The correct view (al-asahh): recite both aloud.
The dispute centres on avoiding combining loud and soft in one rak'ah.
3
Trace the proofs
Examine the evidences: the hadith of Aisha on loud recitation in tahajjud provides a precedent for jahr. The opposing view argues that Fatiha is originally recited softly and cannot be changed, and that surah is supererogatory so its nature is changed.
The dalil_block in this lesson provides the hadith and its analogy.
4
Analyze the tarjih mechanism
Understand the commentator's reasoning: since combining loud and soft in one rak'ah is not legislated, we resort to the lighter option—changing the supererogatory (Fatiha) to loud rather than changing the obligatory (surah). Thus both are recited aloud, with Fatiha first.
The commentator uses the phrase 'al-asahh' and 'al-ashbah' to indicate tarjih.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: an imam following the correct view will recite both Fatiha and surah aloud. Following Abu Hanifa's narration, he recites only the surah aloud; following Muhammad's, he recites both softly.
The mufta bihi is the correct view of reciting both aloud.

كمتنفّل بالليل، ولَوْ ترك السُّورةَ في أُولَيَي العِشَاءِ، قرأها في الأخريين، مع الفاتِحَةِ، جهراً ولـو تـرك الفاتحة لا يُكرَّرُهَا فِي الْأُخْرَيَيْنِ. فيه على حاجة الناس، والمنفرد (كمتنفّل بالليل) فإنه يخير إن شاء جهر وإن شاء أسر، ويكتفي بأدنى الجهر لحصول المقصود به ولا يجهر بما يضر بنائم الحديث عائشة أنه عليه الصلاة والسلام « جَهَرَ فِي التَّهجدِ بالليل، وكَانَ يؤنس اليقظان ولا يوقظ الوسنان » العشاء، وفي الثالثـة مـن المـغــرب (مَـعَ الفَاتِحَةِ، جَهْرَاً) بهما على الأصح. وروى ابن سماعة عن أبي حنيفة: أنه يجهر بالسورة لا الفاتحة ولا يلزم الجمع بين المخافتة والجهر في ركعة، لأن القراءة تلتحق عليه؛ لأنها لم تشرع حقاً له، وأما السورة فشرعت نفلاً في الأخريين حتى لو قرأها فيهما بعد الفاتحة لم يجب عليه سجود السهو فملك صرفها إلى ما عليه.
Like a voluntary night prayer, and if one leaves the surah in the first two of Isha, he recites it in the last two, along with Fatiha, aloud. And if he leaves the Fatiha, he does not repeat it in the last two. Therein regarding the need of people, and the individual (like a voluntary night prayer) he has choice: if he wishes he may recite aloud and if he wishes silently, and he suffices with the minimum of loudness to achieve the purpose, and he does not recite aloud something that harms a sleeping person. The hadith of Aisha that he (peace be upon him) recited aloud in tahajjud at night, and he would comfort the awake and not wake the drowsy. As in Mir'aj al-Diraya. (And if he leaves the surah) in one rak'ah of the first of Maghrib or all of the first of Isha, he recites it, i.e., the surah, the imam necessarily according to the most correct view, and recommendatorily as said in the original, in the last two of Isha, and in the third of Maghrib, along with Fatiha, aloud, both according to the most correct view. And Ibn Sama'a narrated from Abu Hanifa: he recites the surah aloud but not Fatiha, and combining silent and loud in one rak'ah is not required because the recitation attaches to the place of performance, and Fakhr al-Islam chose this. And Hisham narrated from Muhammad that he does not... originally, as for Fatiha, because it is obligatory to recite it silently, and as for surah, so that he does not combine loud and silent in one rak'ah, which is not legislated. And the face of the correct view is that since combining loud and silent is not legislated in one rak'ah, we resort to the lighter of the two matters, which is changing the nature of the supererogatory, which is Fatiha, without changing the nature of the obligatory, which is the surah. And he recites Fatiha first then the surah, and this is the more resembling. And according to some, he recites the surah first because it is attached to the first two, so its precedence is more appropriate. And according to some, he omits Fatiha because it is not obligatory. And if he remembers Fatiha after reciting the surah before bowing, he performs it and repeats the surah in the apparent madhhab, because when he performs it, it becomes obligatory like the surah, so it is as if he remembered the surah while bowing, then he performs it and repeats the bowing. (And if he leaves Fatiha) in the first two, he does not repeat it in the last two according to them, and he performs the prostration of forgetfulness because reciting Fatiha in the second pair is legislated just as reciting a surah in it is also legislated as supererogatory, as in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir by Fakhr al-Islam. So if he recites Fatiha once, it counts as performance because it is stronger due to being in its place, and if he repeats it, he contravenes the legislated practice. Unlike the surah, because the second pair is not a place for its performance, so it may occur as make-up. But al-'Utabi mentioned in his Fatawa that repeating Fatiha in supererogatory prayer is not disliked because of a report similar to it. For if it is said: make-up is diverting what is due to what was due, and Fatiha is legislated in the last two as a right of his, so he can divert it to what was due and thus perform it as make-up, while the surah is not legislated in the last two as a right of his, so he does not perform it as make-up. We say: according to the narration of Hasan from Abu Hanifa, reciting Fatiha is obligatory in the last two, so he does not have the power to divert it to what was due because it was not legislated as a right of his. As for the surah, it is legislated as supererogatory in the last two, so even if he recites it in them after Fatiha, prostration of forgetfulness is not obligatory upon him, so he has the power to divert it to what was due.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the ruling on recitation when a surah is omitted in the first two rak'ahs of a Fard prayer (Maghrib or Isha). The base text (Nur al-Idah) states that if one omits the surah in the first two, he recites it in the last two along with Fatiha, and does so aloud. The commentary explains that this ruling is based on the analogy of voluntary night prayer, where one may choose between loud and silent recitation, as indicated by the hadith of Aisha. For the imam in a congregational Fard prayer, the ruling is obligatory (or recommended) to recite the omitted surah in the last two rak'ahs, and he must recite Fatiha aloud as well, according to the most correct view. A dispute is recorded: Ibn Sama'a narrates from Abu Hanifa that only the surah is recited aloud, not Fatiha, to avoid combining silent and loud in one rak'ah. Fakhr al-Islam chose this view. Hisham narrates from Muhammad that he does not recite aloud at all. The correct view is that since combining is not legislated, we resort to the lighter: changing the nature of the supererogatory (Fatiha) rather than the obligatory (surah). Thus he recites Fatiha first then surah. Some say surah first; some omit Fatiha. The passage then addresses the case of forgetting Fatiha in the first two: it is not repeated in the last two, but one performs prostration of forgetfulness. A subtle point is made using the narration of Hasan from Abu Hanifa that Fatiha is wajib in the last two, so it cannot be diverted to make-up, while surah is supererogatory there, so it can be diverted. This shows the depth of Hanafi legal reasoning.

Key Terms
الجهر (al-jahr): Loud recitation in prayer, audible to others.
المخافتة (al-mukhafatah): Soft recitation, whispered, not loud.
السورة (al-surah): An additional chapter from the Quran after Fatiha, which is sunnah in prayer.
الفاتحة (al-Fatihah): The opening chapter of the Quran, obligatory (fard) in every rak'ah of prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If an imam omits the surah in the first two rak'ahs of Isha, what is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) ruling for recitation in the last two rak'ahs?
A) He recites only the surah aloud, not Fatiha.
B) He recites both Fatiha and the surah aloud.
C) He recites both softly.
D) He does not recite anything, as the omission is compensated by prostration of forgetfulness.

Key Takeaway
When a surah is omitted in the first two rak'ahs, the Hanafi mufta bihi requires reciting both Fatiha and the surah aloud in the last two, based on the principle of avoiding combining jahr and ikhfa by changing the supererogatory element.

Sunnah of Raising Hands for the Opening Takbir
Lists and proves the sunnah of raising hands with gender-specific levels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the sunnah of raising hands for the opening takbir, distinguish the correct hand level for men, slave women, and free women, and explain the evidence.

Core Idea
Raising hands at the opening takbir is sunnah muakkadah; men and slave women raise to ears, free women to shoulders for modesty.

This passage begins a chapter on the sunnahs of prayer. The first sunnah discussed is raising the hands for the opening takbir. While all Hanafis agree it is sunnah, the level to which hands are raised differs based on gender and status. The text provides clear hadith evidence and resolves an intra-madhhab difference for free women.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Raising hands for the opening takbir is ruled as sunnah muakkadah in the Hanafi school.
Not fard or wajib; omitting it does not invalidate prayer.
2
Locate the dalil
Multiple hadiths are cited, including from Sahih Muslim, al-Tahawi, al-Daraqutni, and al-Hakim, showing the Prophet raised his hands to his ears.
The evidence is strong (mutawatir in transmission, zanni in meaning regarding obligation).
3
Distinguish the three categories
Men raise to ears; female slaves also to ears (like men); free women raise to shoulders (correct view).
The difference is based on 'awrah: free woman's forearm is 'awrah, so she raises only to shoulders to conceal it.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
There is a narration from al-Hasan that free woman raises to ears, but the correct view (mufta bihi) is shoulders.
The commentator asserts 'ala al-sahih' (according to the correct view).
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The relied-upon position is from the narration of Muhammad ibn Muqatil from our companions, supported by the principle of concealment.
This is the mufta bihi for fatwa.

فصل في سنن الصلاة (٤) (وَهِيَ إِحْدَى وَخَمْسُونَ) تقريباً، منها أنه يسن: (رَفْعُ اليدين للتحريمة، حذاءَ الأُذنين، للرّجل) لما في صحيح مسلم عن وائل بن حجر أنه رأى النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم رفع يديه حين دخل في الصلاة كـبر ووضعهما حيال أذنيه). ولما روى الطحاوي والدارقطني: كان رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : ) « إِذَا صَلَّى رَفَعَ يَدَيهِ حَتَّى يكونَ إبهامَاهُ حذاء أذُنَيْهِ ) (۳)، زاد الدارقطني فيه: « ثم لم يعد » . وروى هـو والحـاكـم عــن أنـس قال: رأيت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم « كبر فَحَاذَى بِإِبهَامِيهِ أذنيهِ ثُمَّ رَكَعَ حَتَّى اسْتَقَرَّ كُلِّ مِفْصَلٍ مِنْهُ وَانحَطَّ بالتكبير حتَّى سَبَقَتْ يَدَاهُ رُكْبَتَيْهِ » (۳). قال الحاكم: إسناده صحيح على شرط الشيخين. وروى الدارقطني بطريق آخر عن أنس قال : كان رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ) إِذَا افْتَتَحَ الصَّلاةَ كَبَّرَ : ثم رفع يديه حتى يُحَاذِي بِإِبْهَامِيهِ أذنيه ثم يقول: سُبْحَانَكَ اللَّهمَّ وَبِحَمْدِكَ ) إلخ. وقال: رجال إسناده كلهم ثقات (وَ) حذاء أذني (الأمةِ)؛ لأنها كالرجل في الرفع وكالحرة في الركوع والسجود وتنضم لأن ذراعيها ليسا بعورة. (و) رفع اليدين ( حذاءَ المَنْكِبَيْنِ للحرّةِ على الصحيح، لأن مبنى حالها على الستر، وذراعها عورة، وهذه رواية محمد بن مقاتل عن أصحابنا، وفي المجتبى» إلى منكبيها حذاء ثدييها، واحترزنا بالصحيح عن رواية الحسن التي قدمناها أنها ترفع حذاء أذنيها. (وَ) يسن (نَشْرُ الأصابع) لما روي أنه عليه الصلاة والسلام « كَانَ إِذَا كَبَّرَ رَفَعَ يَدَيْهِ نَاشِرَا أَصَابِعَهُ » ) وكيفيته أن لا يضم كل الضم ولا يفرج كل التفريج، بل يتركها على حالها منشورة ( وَ ) : (مُقَارَنَةُ إِحْرَامِ المُقْتَدِي لإحْرَامِ إِمَامِهِ) عند الإمام؛ لأنه عليه الصلاة والسلام أمر المؤتمين بالتكبير في زمــان يـكـبر فيه الإمام بقوله: « إذا كبر فكبروا ) ()، لأن إذا للوقت حقيقة كالحين، فيكون تقديره ره فكبروا في زمان
Chapter on the Sunnahs of Prayer (4). They are approximately fifty-one. Among them it is sunnah: (Raising the hands for the opening takbir, level with the ears, for the man) because of what is in Sahih Muslim from Wa'il ibn Hujr that he saw the Prophet (peace be upon him) raise his hands when he entered prayer, saying the takbir, and placed them parallel to his ears. And because al-Tahawi and al-Daraqutni narrated: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) when he prayed, he raised his hands until his thumbs were level with his ears. Al-Daraqutni added: 'then he did not repeat.' And he and al-Hakim narrated from Anas: I saw the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) say the takbir and then place his thumbs parallel to his ears, then bow until every joint settled, and he descended with the takbir until his hands preceded his knees. Al-Hakim said: Its chain is authentic according to the conditions of the two Shaykhs. And al-Daraqutni narrated by another chain from Anas: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) when he opened the prayer, he said the takbir, then raised his hands until his thumbs were parallel to his ears, then said: 'Subhanaka Allahumma wa bihamdika' etc. He said: All the men of its chain are trustworthy. (And) level with the ears (of the female slave) because she is like the man in raising and like the free woman in bowing and prostration, and she is covered because her forearms are not 'awrah. (And) raising the hands (level with the shoulders for the free woman according to the correct view, because her condition is based on concealment, and her forearm is 'awrah. This is the narration of Muhammad ibn Muqatil from our companions, and in al-Mujtaba: to her shoulders parallel to her breasts. And we guard by the correct view against the narration of al-Hasan which we presented that she raises level with her ears. (And) it is sunnah (spreading the fingers) because it is narrated that he (peace be upon him) when he said the takbir, he raised his hands with his fingers spread. Its manner is that he neither closes them completely nor spreads them completely, but leaves them as they are, spread. (And): (The follower's ihram being concurrent with the ihram of his imam) according to Abu Hanifa; because the Prophet (peace be upon him) commanded the followers to say the takbir at the time when the imam says the takbir by his words: 'When he says the takbir, then say the takbir', because 'idha' (when) is for time in reality like 'hin' (time), so its implied meaning is 'then say takbir in the time of his takbir.'
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage begins a new chapter enumerating the sunnahs of prayer. The author states they are approximately fifty-one and then details the first few. The first sunnah is raising the hands for the opening takbir. For a man, the hands should be raised level with his ears, based on multiple authentic hadith from Sahih Muslim and others. For a female slave (umm walad or similar), she raises hands to her ears like a man, because her forearms are not 'awrah. For a free woman, the correct view is that she raises her hands to her shoulders, not above, due to the requirement of concealment; her forearm is 'awrah and should not be exposed. There is another narration from al-Hasan that she raises to ears, but the correct view is to the shoulders. The second sunnah is spreading the fingers: not closed tightly nor spread wide, but naturally. The third sunnah is that the follower's ihram (entering prayer) should be concurrent with the imam's takbir. This is based on the hadith 'When he says takbir, then say takbir', where 'idha' indicates time, so the follower's takbir should be in the same time as the imam's. This is the view of Abu Hanifa; the Sahibayn may have a different view (not mentioned here). The passage provides strong textual evidence for each sunnah.

Key Terms
رفع اليدين (raf' al-yadayn): Raising the hands up to the ears or shoulders during the opening takbir of prayer.
حذاء الأذنين (hidha' al-udhunain): Level with the ears; the hands should be raised so that the thumbs align with the earlobes.
نشر الأصابع (nashr al-asabi'): Spreading the fingers naturally, not tightly closed nor widely opened.
مقارنة الإحرام (muqaranat al-ihram): The follower's takbirat al-ihram being approximately at the same time as the imam's, not before or after.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the correct Hanafi view, to what level should a free woman raise her hands during the opening takbir?
A) To her ears, like a man.
B) To her shoulders, because her forearm is 'awrah.
C) To her chest.
D) She does not raise her hands at all.

Key Takeaway
Raising hands at the opening takbir is sunnah; the level differs by gender: men and slave women raise to ears, free women to shoulders.

Salah — Prayer
intermediate
7 min
Sunnahs of Spreading Fingers and Follower's Takbir
Lists and proves two sunnahs: finger position and timing of follower's takbir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the sunnah of spreading fingers during the opening takbir and explain the evidence for the follower's takbir being concurrent with the imam's, including the linguistic argument.

Core Idea
Spreading fingers naturally is sunnah; the follower's takbir should be simultaneous with the imam's based on the linguistic nuance of 'idha'.

This passage continues the enumeration of sunnahs by adding two more: the manner of holding the fingers during the opening takbir and the timing of the follower's takbir. The author provides a hadith for the finger position and a unique linguistic argument from Abu Hanifa that the word 'idha' (when) in the hadith 'If he says takbir, then say takbir' implies concurrency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
Spreading fingers is a sunnah; the follower's takbir concurrent with the imam's is also sunnah.
Both are sunnah muakkadah.
2
Locate the dalil for spreading fingers
Hadith: 'When he said takbir, he raised his hands with fingers spread.'
The manner is natural, not closed or overly open.
3
Analyze the dalil for follower's takbir
Hadith: 'If he says takbir, then say takbir.' Abu Hanifa interprets 'idha' as indicating time, so the follower's takbir must occur within the time of the imam's takbir.
This is a linguistic argument specific to the Hanafi school.
4
Understand the practical application
The follower should say takbir roughly at the same time as the imam; not before or after.
If done before or after, the prayer is still valid but the sunnah is missed.
5
Note the mufta bihi
Both positions are mufta bihi in the Hanafi school.
They are considered sunnah muakkadah.

Commentary
This passage begins a new chapter enumerating the sunnahs of prayer. The author states they are approximately fifty-one and then details the first few. The first sunnah is raising the hands for the opening takbir. For a man, the hands should be raised level with his ears, based on multiple authentic hadith from Sahih Muslim and others. For a free woman, the correct view is that she raises her hands to her shoulders, not above, due to the requirement of concealment; her forearm is 'awrah and should not be exposed. There is another narration from al-Hasan that she raises to ears, but the correct view is to the shoulders. The second sunnah is spreading the fingers: not closed tightly nor spread wide, but naturally. The third sunnah is that the follower's ihram (entering prayer) should be concurrent with the imam's takbir. This is based on the hadith 'When he says takbir, then say takbir', where 'idha' indicates time, so the follower's takbir should be in the same time as the imam's. This is the view of Abu Hanifa; the Sahibayn may have a different view (not mentioned here). The passage provides strong textual evidence for each sunnah.

Key Terms

مقارنة الإحرام (muqaranat al-ihram): The follower's takbirat al-ihram being approximately at the same time as the imam's, not before or after.

Checkpoint
What is the basis for the sunnah of the follower's takbir being concurrent with the imam's?
A) Hadith and linguistic analysis of the word 'idha'.
B) Only a weak hadith.
C) Consensus of the companions.
D) The imam's action alone.

Key Takeaway
Spreading fingers naturally and saying takbir with the imam are sunnah acts; the latter is supported by a linguistic argument from Abu Hanifa.
